<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;border-top:1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.1em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Quantum_mechanics" title="Template:Quantum mechanics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Quantum_mechanics" title="Template talk:Quantum mechanics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Quantum_mechanics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Bell's theorem</b> is a "<a href="/wiki/No-go_theorem" title="No-go theorem">no-go theorem</a>" that draws an important distinction between <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> (QM) and the world as described by <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a>. This theorem is named after <a href="/wiki/John_Stewart_Bell" title="John Stewart Bell">John Stewart Bell</a>.</p>
<p>In its simplest form, Bell's theorem states:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C.B._Parker_1994_542_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-C.B._Parker_1994_542-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>No physical theory of <a href="/wiki/Local_hidden_variable_theory" title="Local hidden variable theory">local hidden variables</a> can ever reproduce all of the predictions of quantum mechanics.</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell</a> solid-state physicist <a href="/wiki/David_Mermin" title="David Mermin">David Mermin</a> has described the appraisals of the importance of Bell's theorem in the physics community as ranging from "indifference" to "wild extravagance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Berkeley_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory">Lawrence Berkeley</a> particle physicist <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stapp" title="Henry Stapp">Henry Stapp</a> declared: "Bell's theorem is the most profound discovery of science."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bell's theorem rules out <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_hidden_variable" title="Local hidden variable">local hidden variables</a> as a viable explanation of quantum mechanics (though it still leaves the door open for non-local hidden variables). Bell concluded:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In a theory in which parameters are added to quantum mechanics to determine the results of individual measurements, without changing the statistical predictions, there must be a mechanism whereby the setting of one measuring device can influence the reading of another instrument, however remote. Moreover, the signal involved must propagate instantaneously, so that such a theory could not be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorentz_invariance" title="Lorentz invariance">Lorentz invariant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell1964_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bell1964-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Bell summarized one of the least popular ways to address the theorem, <a href="/wiki/Superdeterminism" title="Superdeterminism">superdeterminism</a>, in a 1985 BBC Radio interview:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>There is a way to escape the inference of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superluminal" title="Superluminal">superluminal</a> speeds and spooky action at a distance. But it involves absolute <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a> in the universe, the complete absence of <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a>. Suppose the world is super-deterministic, with not just inanimate nature running on behind-the-scenes clockwork, but with our behavior, including our belief that we are free to choose to do one experiment rather than another, absolutely predetermined, including the ‘decision’ by the experimenter to carry out one set of measurements rather than another, the difficulty disappears. There is no need for a faster-than-light signal to tell particle <i>A</i> what measurement has been carried out on particle <i>B</i>, because the universe, including particle <i>A</i>, already ‘knows’ what that measurement, and its outcome, will be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_background">Historical background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Historical background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the early 1930s, the philosophical implications of the current interpretations of quantum theory troubled many prominent physicists of the day, including <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>. In a well-known 1935 paper, <a href="/wiki/Boris_Podolsky" title="Boris Podolsky">Boris Podolsky</a> and co-authors Einstein and <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Rosen" title="Nathan Rosen">Nathan Rosen</a> (collectively "EPR") sought to demonstrate by the <a href="/wiki/EPR_paradox" title="EPR paradox">EPR paradox</a> that QM was incomplete. This provided hope that a more-complete (and less-troubling) theory might one day be discovered. But that conclusion rested on the seemingly reasonable assumptions of <i>locality</i> and <i>realism</i> (together called "local realism" or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_hidden_variables" title="Local hidden variables">local hidden variables</a>", often interchangeably). In the vernacular of Einstein: <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_locality" title="Principle of locality">locality</a> meant no instantaneous <a href="/wiki/Quantum_entanglement" title="Quantum entanglement">("spooky") action at a distance</a>; realism meant the moon is there even when not being observed. These assumptions were hotly debated in the physics community, notably between Nobel laureates Einstein and <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a>.</p>
<p>In his groundbreaking 1964 paper, "On the Einstein Podolsky Rosen paradox",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell1964_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bell1964-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> physicist <a href="/wiki/John_Stewart_Bell" title="John Stewart Bell">John Stewart Bell</a> presented an analogy (based on spin measurements on pairs of entangled electrons) to EPR's hypothetical paradox. Using their reasoning, he said, a choice of measurement setting here should not affect the outcome of a measurement there (and vice versa). After providing a mathematical formulation of locality and realism based on this, he showed specific cases where this would be inconsistent with the predictions of QM theory.</p>
<p>In experimental tests following Bell's example, now using <a href="/wiki/Quantum_entanglement" title="Quantum entanglement">quantum entanglement</a> of photons instead of electrons, <a href="/wiki/John_Clauser" title="John Clauser">John Clauser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Freedman" title="Stuart Freedman">Stuart Freedman</a> (1972) and <a href="/wiki/Alain_Aspect" title="Alain Aspect">Alain Aspect</a> <i>et al</i>. (1981) demonstrated that the predictions of QM are correct in this regard, although relying on additional unverifiable assumptions that open <a href="/wiki/Loopholes_in_Bell_test_experiments" title="Loopholes in Bell test experiments">loopholes</a> for local realism.</p>
<p>In October 2015, Hensen and co-workers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-7">[7]</a></sup> reported that they performed a loophole-free Bell test which might force one to reject at least one of the principles of locality, realism, or <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">freedom</a> (the last leads to alternative <a href="/wiki/Superdeterminism" title="Superdeterminism">superdeterministic</a> theories).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Two of these logical possibilities, non-locality and non-realism, correspond to well-developed interpretations of quantum mechanics, and have many supporters; this is not the case for the third logical possibility, non-freedom. Conclusive experimental evidence of the violation of Bell's inequality would drastically reduce the class of acceptable deterministic theories but would not falsify absolute determinism, which was described by Bell himself as “... not just inanimate nature running on behind-the-scenes clockwork, but with our behaviour, including our belief that we are free to choose to do one experiment rather than another, absolutely predetermined.” However, Bell himself considered absolute determinism an implausible solution.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bell's theorem states that any physical theory that incorporates <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_locality#Local_realism" title="Principle of locality">local realism</a> cannot reproduce all the predictions of quantum mechanical theory. Because numerous experiments agree with the predictions of quantum mechanical theory, and show differences between correlations that could not be explained by local hidden variables, the experimental results have been taken by many as refuting the concept of local realism as an explanation of the physical phenomena under test. For a hidden variable theory, if Bell's conditions are correct, the results that agree with quantum mechanical theory appear to indicate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superluminal" title="Superluminal">superluminal</a> effects, in contradiction to the principle of locality.</p>
<p>These three key concepts – locality, realism, freedom – are highly technical and much debated. In particular, the concept of <i>realism</i> is now somewhat different from what it was in discussions in the 1930s. It is more precisely called <i><a href="/wiki/Counterfactual_definiteness" title="Counterfactual definiteness">counterfactual definiteness</a></i>; it means that we may think of outcomes of measurements that were not actually performed as being just as much part of reality as those that were made. <i>Locality</i> is short for <i>local relativistic causality</i>. (Currently accepted <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theories</a> <i>are</i> local in the terminology of the <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_(field_theory)" title="Lagrangian (field theory)">Lagrangian formalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Local_quantum_field_theory" title="Local quantum field theory">axiomatic approach</a>.) <i>Freedom</i> refers to the physical possibility of determining settings on measurement devices independently of the internal state of the physical system being measured.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bell_test_for_spin-half_particles_(entangled_qubits).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="1000" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Bell_test_for_spin-half_particles_%28entangled_qubits%29.svg/450px-Bell_test_for_spin-half_particles_%28entangled_qubits%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Bell_test_for_spin-half_particles_%28entangled_qubits%29.svg/675px-Bell_test_for_spin-half_particles_%28entangled_qubits%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Bell_test_for_spin-half_particles_%28entangled_qubits%29.svg/900px-Bell_test_for_spin-half_particles_%28entangled_qubits%29.svg.png 2x" width="450"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bell_test_for_spin-half_particles_(entangled_qubits).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustration of Bell test for spin-half particles such as electrons. A source produces a <a href="/wiki/Singlet_state" title="Singlet state">singlet</a> pair, one particle is sent to one location, and the other is sent to another location. A <a href="/wiki/Measurement_in_quantum_mechanics" title="Measurement in quantum mechanics">measurement</a> of the entangled property is performed at various angles at each location. The scheme for measurements on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photons" title="Photons">photons</a> looks very similar: the quantum state is different but has very similar properties.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The theorem is usually proved by consideration of a quantum system of two <a href="/wiki/Quantum_entanglement" title="Quantum entanglement">entangled</a> <a href="/wiki/Qubit" title="Qubit">qubits</a>. The most common examples concern systems of particles that are entangled in <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a>. Quantum mechanics allows predictions of correlations that would be observed if these two particles have their spin or polarization measured in different directions. Bell showed that if a local hidden variable theory holds, then these correlations would have to satisfy certain constraints, called Bell inequalities. However, for the quantum correlations arising in the specific example considered, those constraints are not satisfied, hence the phenomenon being studied cannot be explained by a local hidden variables theory.</p>
<p>Following the argument in the <a href="/wiki/EPR_paradox" title="EPR paradox">Einstein–Podolsky–Rosen (EPR) paradox</a> paper (but using the example of spin, as in <a href="/wiki/David_Bohm" title="David Bohm">David Bohm</a>'s version of the EPR argument<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell1964_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bell1964-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>), Bell considered an experiment in which there are "a pair of spin one-half particles formed somehow in the <a href="/wiki/Singlet_state" title="Singlet state">singlet spin state</a> and moving freely in opposite directions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell1964_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bell1964-4">[4]</a></sup> The two particles travel away from each other to two distant locations, at which measurements of spin are performed, along axes that are independently chosen. Each measurement yields a result of either spin-up (+) or spin-down (−); it means, spin in the positive or negative direction of the chosen axis.</p>
<p>The probability of the same result being obtained at the two locations depends on the relative angles at which the two spin measurements are made, and is strictly between zero and one for all relative angles other than perfectly parallel or antiparallel alignments (0° or 180°). Since total angular momentum is conserved, and since the total spin is zero in the singlet state, the probability of the same result with parallel (antiparallel) alignment is 0 (1). This last prediction is true classically as well as quantum mechanically.</p>
<p>Bell's theorem is concerned with correlations defined in terms of averages taken over very many trials of the experiment. The <a href="/wiki/Quantum_correlation" title="Quantum correlation">correlation</a> of two binary variables is usually defined in quantum physics as the average of the products of the pairs of measurements. Note that this is different from the usual definition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Correlation" title="Correlation">correlation</a> in statistics. The quantum physicist's "correlation" is the statistician's "raw (uncentered, unnormalized) product <a href="/wiki/Moment_(mathematics)" title="Moment (mathematics)">moment</a>". They are similar in that, with either definition, if the pairs of outcomes are always the same, the correlation is +1; if the pairs of outcomes are always opposite, the correlation is -1; and if the pairs of outcomes agree 50% of the time, then the correlation is 0. The correlation is related in a simple way to the probability of equal outcomes, namely it is equal to twice the probability of equal outcomes, minus one.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pauli_operator" title="Pauli operator">Measuring the spin</a> of these entangled particles along anti-parallel directions—i.e., along the same axis but in opposite directions, the set of all results is perfectly correlated. On the other hand, if measurements are performed along parallel directions they always yield opposite results, and the set of measurements shows perfect anti-correlation. This is in accord with the above stated probabilities of measuring the same result in these two cases. Finally, measurement at perpendicular directions has a 50% chance of matching, and the total set of measurements is uncorrelated. These basic cases are illustrated in the table below. Columns should be read as <i>examples</i> of pairs of values that could be recorded by Alice and Bob with time increasing going to the right.</p>
<table style="float:center">

</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bell.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="630" height="343" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Bell.svg/400px-Bell.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Bell.svg/600px-Bell.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Bell.svg/800px-Bell.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bell.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The best possible local realist imitation (red) for the quantum correlation of two spins in the singlet state (blue), insisting on perfect anti-correlation at zero degrees, perfect correlation at 180 degrees. Many other possibilities exist for the classical correlation subject to these side conditions, but all are characterized by sharp peaks (and valleys) at 0, 180, 360 degrees, and none has more extreme values (<span class="texhtml">±0.5</span>) at 45, 135, 225, 315 degrees. These values are marked by stars in the graph, and are the values measured in a standard Bell-CHSH type experiment: QM allows ±1/<span class="nowrap">√<span style="border-top:1px solid; padding:0 0.1em;">2</span></span> = ±0.7071..., local realism predicts <span class="texhtml">±0.5</span> or less.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the measurements oriented at intermediate angles between these basic cases, the existence of local hidden variables could agree with a linear dependence of the <a href="/wiki/Quantum_correlation" title="Quantum correlation">correlation</a> in the angle but, according to Bell's inequality (see below), could not agree with the dependence predicted by quantum mechanical theory, namely, that the correlation is the negative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosine" title="Cosine">cosine</a> of the angle. Experimental results match the curve predicted by quantum mechanics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C.B._Parker_1994_542_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-C.B._Parker_1994_542-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over the years, Bell's theorem has undergone a wide variety of experimental tests. However, various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bell_test_loopholes" title="Bell test loopholes">common deficiencies in the testing of the theorem</a> have been identified, including the <i>detection loophole</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford-9">[9]</a></sup> and the <i>communication loophole</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford-9">[9]</a></sup> Over the years experiments have been gradually improved to better address these loopholes. In 2015, the first experiment to simultaneously address all of the loopholes was performed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>To date, Bell's theorem is generally regarded as supported by a substantial body of evidence and there are few supporters of local hidden variables, though the theorem is continually the subject of study, criticism, and refinement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Importance_of_the_theorem">Importance of the theorem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Importance of the theorem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bell's theorem, derived in his seminal 1964 paper titled <i>On the Einstein Podolsky Rosen paradox</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell1964_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bell1964-4">[4]</a></sup> has been called, on the assumption that the theory is correct, "the most profound in science".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Perhaps of equal importance is Bell's deliberate effort to encourage and bring legitimacy to work on the completeness issues, which had fallen into disrepute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell_1982_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bell_1982-13">[13]</a></sup> Later in his life, Bell expressed his hope that such work would "continue to inspire those who suspect that what is proved by the impossibility proofs is lack of imagination."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell_1982_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bell_1982-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The title of Bell's seminal article refers to the 1935 paper by <a href="/wiki/EPR_paradox" title="EPR paradox">Einstein, Podolsky and Rosen</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> that challenged the completeness of quantum mechanics. In his paper, Bell started from the same two assumptions as did EPR, namely (i) <i>reality</i> (that microscopic objects have real properties determining the outcomes of quantum mechanical measurements), and (ii) <i>locality</i> (that reality in one location is not influenced by measurements performed simultaneously at a distant location). Bell was able to derive from those two assumptions an important result, namely Bell's inequality. The theoretical (and later experimental) violation of this inequality implies that at least one of the two assumptions must be false.</p>
<p>In two respects Bell's 1964 paper was a step forward compared to the EPR paper: firstly, it considered more <a href="/wiki/Hidden_variable_theory" title="Hidden variable theory">hidden variables</a> than merely the <a href="/wiki/EPR_paradox" title="EPR paradox">element of physical reality</a> in the EPR paper; and Bell's inequality was, in part, liable to be experimentally tested, thus raising the possibility of testing the local realism hypothesis. Limitations on such tests to date are noted below. Whereas Bell's paper deals only with deterministic hidden variable theories, Bell's theorem was later generalized to <a href="/wiki/Stochastic" title="Stochastic">stochastic</a> theories<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clauser.E2.80.93Horne_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clauser.E2.80.93Horne-15">[15]</a></sup> as well, and it was also realised<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> that the theorem is not so much about hidden variables, as about the outcomes of measurements that could have been taken instead of the one actually taken. Existence of these variables is called the assumption of realism, or the assumption of <a href="/wiki/Counterfactual_definiteness" title="Counterfactual definiteness">counterfactual definiteness</a>.</p>
<p>After the EPR paper, quantum mechanics was in an unsatisfactory position: either it was incomplete, in the sense that it failed to account for some elements of physical reality, or it violated the principle of a finite propagation speed of physical effects. In a modified version of the EPR thought experiment, two hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Observer_(quantum_physics)" title="Observer (quantum physics)">observers</a>, now commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Alice_and_Bob" title="Alice and Bob"><i>Alice</i> and <i>Bob</i></a>, perform independent measurements of spin on a pair of electrons, prepared at a source in a special state called a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin_singlet" title="Spin singlet">spin singlet</a> state</i>. It is the conclusion of EPR that once Alice measures spin in one direction (e.g. on the <i>x</i> axis), Bob's measurement in that direction is determined with certainty, as being the opposite outcome to that of Alice, whereas immediately before Alice's measurement Bob's outcome was only statistically determined (i.e., was only a probability, not a certainty); thus, either the spin in each direction is an <i>element of physical reality</i>, or the effects travel from Alice to Bob instantly.</p>
<p>In QM, predictions are formulated in terms of <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probabilities</a> — for example, the probability that an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> will be detected in a particular place, or the probability that its spin is up or down. The idea persisted, however, that the electron in fact has a <i>definite</i> position and spin, and that QM's weakness is its inability to predict those values precisely. The possibility existed that some unknown theory, such as a <i>hidden variables theory</i>, might be able to predict those quantities exactly, while at the same time also being in complete agreement with the probabilities predicted by QM. If such a hidden variables theory exists, then because the hidden variables are not described by QM the latter would be an incomplete theory.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_realism">Local realism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Local realism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The concept of local realism is formalized to state and prove Bell's theorem and generalizations. A common approach is the following:</p>
<ol>
<li>There is a <a href="/wiki/Probability_space" title="Probability space">probability space</a> <span class="texhtml">Λ</span> and the observed outcomes by both Alice and Bob result by random sampling of the (unknown, "hidden") parameter <span class="texhtml"><i>λ</i> ∈ Λ</span>.</li>
<li>The values observed by Alice or Bob are functions of the local detector settings and the hidden parameter only. Thus, there are functions <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i>,<i>B</i> : <i>S</i><sup>2</sup> × Λ → {-1, +1}</span> , where a detector setting is modeled as a location on the unit sphere <span class="texhtml"><i>S</i><sup>2</sup></span>, such that
<ul>
<li>The value observed by Alice with detector setting <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span> is <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i>(<i>a</i>, <i>λ</i>)</span></li>
<li>The value observed by Bob with detector setting <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span> is <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i>(<i>b</i>, <i>λ</i>)</span></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Perfect anti-correlation would require <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i>(<i>c</i>, <i>λ</i>) = −<i>A</i>(<i>c</i>, <i>λ</i>), <i>c</i> ∈ <i>S</i><sup>2</sup></span>. Implicit in assumption 1) above, the hidden parameter space <span class="texhtml">Λ</span> has a <a href="/wiki/Probability_measure" title="Probability measure">probability measure</a> <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">μ</span> and the <a href="/wiki/Expectation_value_(quantum_mechanics)" title="Expectation value (quantum mechanics)">expectation</a> of a random variable <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">X</span> on <span class="texhtml">Λ</span> with respect to <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">μ</span> is written</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \operatorname {E} (X)=\int _{\Lambda }X(\lambda )p(\lambda )d\lambda ,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6b33e805a4d6cb64930f24e2436ce3b87deb2f94" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:24.405ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where for accessibility of notation we assume that the probability measure has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Probability_density" title="Probability density">probability density</a> <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">p</span> that therefore is nonnegative and integrates to <span class="texhtml">1</span>. The hidden parameter is often thought of as being associated with the source but it can just as well also contain components associated with the two measurement devices.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bell_inequalities">Bell inequalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bell inequalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bell inequalities concern measurements made by observers on pairs of particles that have interacted and then separated. Assuming local realism, certain constraints must hold on the relationships between the correlations between subsequent measurements of the particles under various possible measurement settings. </math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C_{h}(a,b)=E(A(\mathbf {a} ,\lambda ),B(\mathbf {b} ,\lambda ))=\int _{\Lambda }A(\mathbf {a} ,\lambda )B(\mathbf {b} ,\lambda )p(\lambda )d\lambda .}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bea8903fce591348677cf35c80b24a2903e50e6b" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:59.184ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
</dd>

<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;">
<div class="NavHead" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;color:green;background:lightgrey;">Details on calculation of <span class="texhtml"><i>C</i><sub><i>q</i></sub>(<b>a</b>, <b>b</b>)</span> </div>
<div class="NavContent" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;">
<p>The two-particle spin space is the <a href="/wiki/Tensor_product" title="Tensor product">tensor product</a> of the two-dimensional spin Hilbert spaces of the individual particles. Each individual space is an <a href="/wiki/Irreducible_representation" title="Irreducible representation">irreducible representation space</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Rotation_group_SO(3)" title="Rotation group SO(3)">rotation group SO(3)</a>. The product space decomposes as a direct sum of irreducible representations with definite total spins <span class="texhtml">0</span> and <span class="texhtml">1</span> of dimensions <span class="texhtml">1</span> and <span class="texhtml">3</span> respectively. Full details may be found in <a href="/wiki/Clebsch%E2%80%93Gordan_coefficients" title="Clebsch–Gordan coefficients">Clebsch—Gordan decompostion</a>. The total spin zero subspace is spanned by the <a href="/wiki/Singlet_state" title="Singlet state">singlet state</a> in the product space, a vector explicitly given by</p>

<p>Given this, the expectation value reduces to</p>

</div>
<p>With this notation, a concise summary of what follows can be made.</p>
<ul>
<li>Theoretically, there exists <span class="texhtml"><b>a</b>, <b>b</b></span> such that</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>q</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>≠<!-- ≠ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>h</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C_{q}\neq C_{h},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C_{q}\neq C_{h},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cba07af9ec7b951c66ee8b06093232b7f16fc07e" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:9.293ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
<dd>whatever are the particular characteristics of the hidden variable theory as long as it abides to the rules of local realism as defined above. That is to say, no hidden variable theory can make the same predictions as quantum mechanics.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Experimentally, instances of</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>e</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>≠<!-- ≠ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>h</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C_{e}\neq C_{h}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C_{e}\neq C_{h}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/df3ebaef157024c78e58d0edec98dacae5ede2b1" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:8.646ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
<dd>have been found (whatever the hidden variable theory), but
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>e</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>≠<!-- ≠ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>q</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mspace width="1em"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>(not found)</mtext>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C_{e}\neq C_{q}\quad {\text{(not found)}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C_{e}\neq C_{q}\quad {\text{(not found)}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7901f975f44925b8f0d68c7a70bb9fb3c2510feb" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:22.384ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
<dd>has never been found. That is to say, predictions of quantum mechanics have never been falsified by experiment. These experiments include such that can rule out hidden variable theories. But see below on possible loopholes.</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_Bell.27s_inequality">Original Bell's inequality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Original Bell's inequality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The inequality that Bell derived can then be written as:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell1964_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bell1964-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>h</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>h</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>b</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>h</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>b</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>≤<!-- ≤ --></mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C_{h}(a,c)-C_{h}(b,a)-C_{h}(b,c)\leq 1,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C_{h}(a,c)-C_{h}(b,a)-C_{h}(b,c)\leq 1,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c412b7acd2a3a2029ba09090e5fa35139a4e6e16" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:34.372ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a, b</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">c</span> refer to three arbitrary settings of the two analysers. This inequality is however restricted in its application to the rather special case in which the outcomes on both sides of the experiment are always exactly anticorrelated whenever the analysers are parallel. The advantage of restricting attention to this special case is the resulting simplicity of the derivation. In experimental work the inequality is not very useful because it is hard, if not impossible, to create <i>perfect</i> anti-correlation.</p>
<p>This simple form does have the virtue of being quite intuitive. It is easily seen to be equivalent to the following elementary result from probability theory. Consider three (highly correlated, and possibly biased) coin-flips <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">X, Y</span>, and <i>Z</i>, with the property that:</p>
<ol>
<li><i>X</i> and <i>Y</i> give the same outcome (both heads or both tails) 99% of the time</li>
<li><i>Y</i> and <i>Z</i> also give the same outcome 99% of the time,</li>
</ol>
<p>then <i>X</i> and <i>Z</i> must also yield the same outcome at least 98% of the time. The number of mismatches between <i>X</i> and <i>Y</i> (1/100) plus the number of mismatches between <i>Y</i> and <i>Z</i> (1/100) are together the <i>maximum possible</i> number of mismatches between <i>X</i> and <i>Z</i> (a simple <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9chet_inequalities" title="Fréchet inequalities">Boole–Fréchet inequality</a>).</p>
<p>Imagine a pair of particles that can be measured at distant locations. Suppose that the measurement devices have settings, which are angles—e.g., the devices measure something called spin in some direction. The experimenter chooses the directions, one for each particle, separately. Suppose the measurement outcome is binary (e.g., spin up, spin down). Suppose the two particles are perfectly anti-correlated—in the sense that whenever both measured in the same direction, one gets identically opposite outcomes, when both measured in opposite directions they always give the same outcome. The only way to imagine how this works is that both particles leave their common source with, somehow, the outcomes they will deliver when measured in any possible direction. (How else could particle 1 know how to deliver the same answer as particle 2 when measured in the same direction? They don't know in advance how they are going to be measured...). The measurement on particle 2 (after switching its sign) can be thought of as telling us what the same measurement on particle 1 would have given.</p>
<p>Start with one setting exactly opposite to the other. All the pairs of particles give the same outcome (each pair is either both spin up or both spin down). Now shift Alice's setting by one degree relative to Bob's. They are now one degree off being exactly opposite to one another. A small fraction of the pairs, say <i>f</i>, now give different outcomes. If instead we had left Alice's setting unchanged but shifted Bob's by one degree (in the opposite direction), then again a fraction <i>f</i> of the pairs of particles turns out to give different outcomes. Finally consider what happens when both shifts are implemented at the same time: the two settings are now exactly two degrees away from being opposite to one another. By the mismatch argument, the chance of a mismatch at two degrees can't be more than twice the chance of a mismatch at one degree: it cannot be more than 2<i>f</i>.</p>
<p>Compare this with the predictions from quantum mechanics for the singlet state. For a small angle <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">θ</span>, measured in radians, the chance of a different outcome is approximately <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f_{1}=\theta ^{2}/2}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle f_{1}=\theta ^{2}/2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/befaf6d185df61108a850df219e1284eac813b0d" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:9.829ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span> as explained by <a href="/wiki/Small-angle_approximation" title="Small-angle approximation">small-angle approximation</a>. At two times this small angle, the chance of a mismatch is therefore about 4 times larger, since <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<msup>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mn>2</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<msup>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
<mo>≈<!-- ≈ --></mo>
<mn>4</mn>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f_{2}=(2\theta )^{2}/2=2^{2}\theta ^{2}/2\approx 4f_{1}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle f_{2}=(2\theta )^{2}/2=2^{2}\theta ^{2}/2\approx 4f_{1}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c117ca4f6d792f859668c15d94da616573281553" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:29.177ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>. But we just argued that it cannot be more than 2 times as large.</p>
<p>This intuitive formulation is due to <a href="/wiki/David_Mermin" title="David Mermin">David Mermin</a>. The small-angle limit is discussed in Bell's original article, and therefore goes right back to the origin of the Bell inequalities.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CHSH_inequality">CHSH inequality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: CHSH inequality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/CHSH_inequality" title="CHSH inequality">CHSH inequality</a></div>
<p>Generalizing Bell's original inequality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell1964_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bell1964-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Clauser" title="John Clauser">John Clauser</a>, Michael Horne, <a href="/wiki/Abner_Shimony" title="Abner Shimony">Abner Shimony</a> and R. A. Holt introduced the <a href="/wiki/CHSH_inequality" title="CHSH inequality">CHSH inequality</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clauser_1969_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clauser_1969-17">[17]</a></sup> which puts classical limits on the set of four correlations in Alice and Bob's experiment, without any assumption of perfect correlations (or anti-correlations) at equal settings</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Derivation_of_CHSH_inequality">Derivation of CHSH inequality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Derivation of CHSH inequality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>With abbreviated notation</p>

<p>the CHSH inequality can be derived as follows. Each of the four quantities is <span class="texhtml">±1</span> and each depends on <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">λ</span>. It follows that for any <span class="texhtml"><i>λ</i> ∈ Λ</span>, one of <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i> + <i>B</i>′</span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i> − <i>B</i>′</span> is zero, and the other is <span class="texhtml">±2</span>. From this it follows that</p>

<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle AB+AB'+A'B-A'B'=A(B+B')+A'(B-B')\leq 2,}</annotation>

<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mi>p</mi>
<mi>d</mi>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mo>≤<!-- ≤ --></mo>
<mn>2.</mn>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}C_{h}(a,b)+C_{h}(a,b')+C_{h}(a',b)-C_{h}(a',b')&amp;=\int _{\Lambda }ABpd\lambda +\int _{\Lambda }AB'pd\lambda +\int _{\Lambda }A'Bpd\lambda -\int _{\Lambda }A'B'pd\lambda \\&amp;=\int _{\Lambda }(AB+AB'+A'B-A'B')pd\lambda \\&amp;=\int _{\Lambda }(A(B+B')+A'(B-B'))pd\lambda \leq 2.\end{aligned}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}C_{h}(a,b)+C_{h}(a,b')+C_{h}(a',b)-C_{h}(a',b')&amp;=\int _{\Lambda }ABpd\lambda +\int _{\Lambda }AB'pd\lambda +\int _{\Lambda }A'Bpd\lambda -\int _{\Lambda }A'B'pd\lambda \\&amp;=\int _{\Lambda }(AB+AB'+A'B-A'B')pd\lambda \\&amp;=\int _{\Lambda }(A(B+B')+A'(B-B'))pd\lambda \leq 2.\end{aligned}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/23f34f50f7c61d31a1bbaf3ee7a160fca32862d7" style="vertical-align: -8.338ex; width:99.909ex; height:17.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>At the heart of this derivation is a simple algebraic inequality concerning four variables, <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i>, <i>A</i>′, <i>B</i>, <i>B</i>′</span>, which take the values <span class="texhtml">±1</span> only:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mn>2.</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle AB+AB'+A'B-A'B'=A(B+B')+A'(B-B')\leq 2.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="AB+AB'+A'B-A'B'=A(B+B')+A'(B-B')\leq 2." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/39921be7c6ecd6a096db4098ad0c5c752a0fa8d7" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:58.375ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The CHSH inequality is seen to depend only on the following three key features of a local hidden variables theory: (1) realism: alongside of the outcomes of actually performed measurements, the outcomes of potentially performed measurements also exist at the same time; (2) locality, the outcomes of measurements on Alice's particle don't depend on which measurement Bob chooses to perform on the other particle; (3) freedom: Alice and Bob can indeed choose freely which measurements to perform.</p>
<p>The <i>realism</i> assumption is actually somewhat idealistic, and Bell's theorem only proves non-locality with respect to variables that only <i>exist</i> for metaphysical reasons. However, before the discovery of quantum mechanics, both realism and locality were completely uncontroversial features of physical theories.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bell_inequalities_are_violated_by_quantum_mechanical_predictions">Bell inequalities are violated by quantum mechanical predictions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bell inequalities are violated by quantum mechanical predictions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The measurements performed by Alice and Bob are spin measurements on electrons. Alice can choose between two detector settings labeled <i>a</i> and <i>a</i>′; these settings correspond to measurement of spin along the <i>z</i> or the <i>x</i> axis. Bob can choose between two detector settings labeled <i>b</i> and <i>b</i>′; these correspond to measurement of spin along the <i>z</i>′ or <i>x</i>′ axis, where the <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i>′ − <i>z</i>′</span> coordinate system is rotated 135° relative to the <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i> − <i>z</i></span> coordinate system. The spin observables are represented by the 2 × 2 self-adjoint matrices:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>so that</p>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \langle A(a)B(b)\rangle +\langle A(a')B(b')\rangle +\langle A(a')B(b)\rangle -\langle A(a)B(b')\rangle ={\tfrac {4}{\sqrt {2}}}=2{\sqrt {2}}&gt;2}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\langle A(a)B(b)\rangle +\langle A(a')B(b')\rangle +\langle A(a')B(b)\rangle -\langle A(a)B(b')\rangle ={\tfrac {4}{\sqrt {2}}}=2{\sqrt {2}}&gt;2" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e613cac2fcb2c21df4621d4a97917e67a86975a2" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:74.222ex; height:4.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Bell's Theorem: If the quantum mechanical formalism is correct, then the system consisting of a pair of entangled electrons cannot satisfy the principle of local realism. Note that <span class="texhtml">2<span class="nowrap">√<span style="border-top:1px solid; padding:0 0.1em;">2</span></span></span> is indeed the upper bound for quantum mechanics called <a href="/wiki/Tsirelson%27s_bound" title="Tsirelson's bound">Tsirelson's bound</a>. The operators giving this maximal value are always <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isomorphic" title="Isomorphic">isomorphic</a> to the Pauli matrices.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practical_experiments_testing_Bell.27s_theorem">Practical experiments testing Bell's theorem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Practical experiments testing Bell's theorem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bell-test-photon-analyer.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="1232" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Bell-test-photon-analyer.png/450px-Bell-test-photon-analyer.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Bell-test-photon-analyer.png/675px-Bell-test-photon-analyer.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Bell-test-photon-analyer.png/900px-Bell-test-photon-analyer.png 2x" width="450"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bell-test-photon-analyer.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<b>Scheme of a "two-channel" Bell test</b><br/>
The source S produces pairs of "photons", sent in opposite directions. Each photon encounters a two-channel polariser whose orientation (a or b) can be set by the experimenter. Emerging signals from each channel are detected and coincidences of four types (++, −−, +− and −+) counted by the coincidence monitor.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bell_test_experiments" title="Bell test experiments">Bell test experiments</a></div>
<p>Experimental tests can determine whether the Bell inequalities required by local realism hold up to the empirical evidence.</p>
<p>Actually, most experiments have been performed using polarization of photons rather than spin of electrons (or other spin-half particles). The quantum state of the pair of entangled photons is not the singlet state, and the correspondence between angles and outcomes is different from that in the spin-half set-up. The polarization of a photon is measured in a pair of perpendicular directions. Relative to a given orientation, polarization is either vertical (denoted by V or by +) or horizontal (denoted by H or by -). The photon pairs are generated in the quantum state</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<msqrt>
<mn>2</mn>
</msqrt>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
<mi>V</mi>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟩<!-- ⟩ --></mo>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>H</mi>

</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{\sqrt {2}}}\left(|V\rangle \otimes |V\rangle +|H\rangle \otimes |H\rangle \right)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {1}{\sqrt {2}}}\left(|V\rangle \otimes |V\rangle +|H\rangle \otimes |H\rangle \right)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a2c137ccc00006c37c1c7c527854f0b46e180343" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:27.845ex; height:4.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>V</mi>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle |V\rangle }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="|V\rangle " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/89463c7bdd8be86eae354b0fd9fc5ca09d423499" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.37ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>H</mi>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟩<!-- ⟩ --></mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle |H\rangle }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="|H\rangle " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f010e5c1fd1410fa37d26b3ae7c769db1df7ab57" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.646ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> denotes the state of a single vertically or horizontally polarized photon, respectively (relative to a fixed and common reference direction for both particles).</p>
<p>When the polarization of both photons is measured in the same direction, both give the same outcome: perfect correlation. When measured at directions making an angle 45 degrees with one another, the outcomes are completely random (uncorrelated). Measuring at directions at 90 degrees to one another, the two are perfectly anti-correlated. In general, when the polarizers are at an angle <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">θ</span> to one another, the correlation is <span class="texhtml">cos(2<i>θ</i>)</span>. So relative to the correlation function for the singlet state of spin half particles, we have a positive rather than a negative cosine function, and angles are halved: the correlation is periodic with period <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> instead of <span class="texhtml">2<i>π</i></span>.</p>
<p>Bell's inequalities are tested by "coincidence counts" from a Bell test experiment such as the optical one shown in the diagram. Pairs of particles are emitted as a result of a quantum process, analysed with respect to some key property such as polarisation direction, then detected. The setting (orientations) of the analysers are selected by the experimenter.</p>
<p>Bell test experiments to date overwhelmingly violate Bell's inequality.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Two_classes_of_Bell_inequalities">Two classes of Bell inequalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Two classes of Bell inequalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>fair sampling</i> problem was faced openly in the 1970s. In early designs of their 1973 experiment, Freedman and Clauser<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clauser.E2.80.93Freedman_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clauser.E2.80.93Freedman-18">[18]</a></sup> used <i>fair sampling</i> in the form of the Clauser–Horne–Shimony–Holt (CHSH<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clauser_1969_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clauser_1969-17">[17]</a></sup>) hypothesis. However, shortly afterwards Clauser and Horne<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clauser.E2.80.93Horne_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clauser.E2.80.93Horne-15">[15]</a></sup> made the important distinction between inhomogeneous (IBI) and homogeneous (HBI) Bell inequalities. Testing an IBI requires that we compare certain coincidence rates in two separated detectors with the singles rates of the two detectors. Nobody needed to perform the experiment, because singles rates with all detectors in the 1970s were at least ten times all the coincidence rates. So, taking into account this low detector efficiency, the QM prediction actually satisfied the IBI. To arrive at an experimental design in which the QM prediction violates IBI we require detectors whose efficiency exceeds 82.8% for singlet states,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garg_.26_Mermin.2C_1987_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garg_.26_Mermin.2C_1987-19">[19]</a></sup> but have very low dark rate and short dead and resolving times. This is now within reach.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Practical_challenges">Practical challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Practical challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Loopholes_in_Bell_test_experiments" title="Loopholes in Bell test experiments">Loopholes in Bell test experiments</a></div>
<p>Because, at that time, even the best detectors didn't detect a large fraction of all photons, Clauser and Horne<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clauser.E2.80.93Horne_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clauser.E2.80.93Horne-15">[15]</a></sup> recognized that testing Bell's inequality required some extra assumptions. They introduced the <i>No Enhancement Hypothesis</i> (NEH):</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>A light signal, originating in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_cascade" title="Atomic cascade">atomic cascade</a> for example, has a certain probability of activating a detector. Then, if a polarizer is interposed between the cascade and the detector, the detection probability cannot increase.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Given this assumption, there is a Bell inequality between the coincidence rates with polarizers and coincidence rates without polarizers.</p>
<p>The experiment was performed by Freedman and Clauser,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clauser.E2.80.93Freedman_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clauser.E2.80.93Freedman-18">[18]</a></sup> who found that the Bell's inequality was violated. So the no-enhancement hypothesis cannot be true in a local hidden variables model.</p>
<p>While early experiments used atomic cascades, later experiments have used parametric down-conversion, following a suggestion by Reid and Walls,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> giving improved generation and detection properties. As a result, the most recent experiments with photons no longer suffer from the detection loophole (see <a href="/wiki/Bell_test_experiments" title="Bell test experiments">Bell test experiments</a>). This makes the photon the first experimental system for which all main experimental loopholes have been surmounted, albeit presently only in separate experiments (Giustina et al. (2013), <i>Bell violation using entangled photons without the fair-sampling assumption</i>, Nature 497, 227–230; B.G. Christensen et al. (2013), <i>Detection-Loophole-Free Test of Quantum Nonlocality, and Applications</i>, arXiv:1306.5772).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysical_aspects">Metaphysical aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Metaphysical aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Most advocates of the hidden-variables idea believe that experiments have ruled out local hidden variables. They are ready to give up locality, explaining the violation of Bell's inequality by means of a non-local <a href="/wiki/Hidden_variable_theory" title="Hidden variable theory">hidden variable theory</a>, in which the particles exchange information about their states. This is the basis of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bohm_interpretation" title="Bohm interpretation">Bohm interpretation</a> of quantum mechanics, which requires that all particles in the universe be able to instantaneously exchange information with all others. A 2007 experiment ruled out a large class of non-Bohmian non-local hidden variable theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>If the hidden variables can communicate with each other faster than light, Bell's inequality can easily be violated. Once one particle is measured, it can communicate the necessary correlations to the other particle. Since in relativity the notion of simultaneity is not absolute, this is unattractive. One idea is to replace instantaneous communication with a process that travels backwards in time along the past <a href="/wiki/Light_cone" title="Light cone">light cone</a>. This is the idea behind a <a href="/wiki/Transactional_interpretation" title="Transactional interpretation">transactional interpretation</a> of quantum mechanics, which interprets the statistical emergence of a quantum history as a gradual coming to agreement between histories that go both forward and backward in time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>A few advocates of deterministic models have not given up on local hidden variables. For example, <a href="/wiki/Gerard_%27t_Hooft" title="Gerard 't Hooft">Gerard 't Hooft</a> has argued that the <a href="/wiki/Superdeterminism" title="Superdeterminism">superdeterminism</a> loophole cannot be dismissed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>A possible (but not universally accepted) solution is offered by the <a href="/wiki/Many-worlds_interpretation" title="Many-worlds interpretation">many worlds theory</a> of quantum mechanics. According to this, not only is collapse of the wave function illusory, but the apparent random branching of possible futures when quantum systems interact with the macroscopic world is also an illusion. Measurement does not lead to a random choice of possible outcome; rather, the only ingredient of quantum mechanics is the unitary evolution of the wave function. All possibilities co-exist forever and the only reality is the quantum mechanical wave function. According to this view, two distant observers both split into superpositions when measuring a spin. The Bell inequality violations are no longer counterintuitive, because it is not clear which copy of the observer B will be seen by observer A when they compare notes. If reality includes all the different outcomes, locality in physical space (not outcome space) places no restrictions on how the split observers can meet up.</p>
<p>This point underlines the fact that the argument that realism is incompatible with quantum mechanics and locality depends on a particular formalization of the concept of realism. In its weakest form, the assumption underpinning that particular formalization is called <a href="/wiki/Counterfactual_definiteness" title="Counterfactual definiteness">counterfactual definiteness</a>. This is the assumption that outcomes of measurements that are not performed are just as real as those of measurements that were performed. Counterfactual definiteness is an uncontroversial property of all classical physical theories prior to quantum theory, due to their determinism. Many worlds interpretations are not only counterfactually indefinite, but are also factually indefinite. The results of all experiments, even ones that have been performed, are not uniquely determined.</p>
<p>If one chooses to reject counterfactual definiteness, reality has been made smaller, and there is no non-locality problem. On the other hand, one is thereby introducing irreducible or intrinsic randomness into our picture of the world: randomness that cannot be "explained" as merely the reflection of our ignorance of underlying, variable, physical quantities. Non-determinism becomes a fundamental property of nature.</p>
<p>Assuming counterfactual definiteness, reality has been enlarged, and there is a non-locality problem. On the other hand, in the many-worlds interpretation of quantum mechanics, reality consists only of a deterministically evolving wave function and non-locality is a non-issue.</p>
<p>There have also been repeated claims that Bell's arguments are irrelevant because they depend on hidden assumptions that, in fact, are questionable. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._T._Jaynes" title="E. T. Jaynes">E. T. Jaynes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> claimed in 1989 that there are two hidden assumptions in Bell's theorem that could limit its generality. According to him:</p>
<ol>
<li>Bell interpreted conditional probability P(X|Y) as a causal inference, i.e. Y exerted a causal inference on X in reality. However, P(X|Y) actually only means logical inference (induction). Causes cannot travel faster than light or backward in time, but deduction can.</li>
<li>Bell's inequality does not apply to some possible hidden variable theories. It only applies to a certain class of local hidden variable theories. In fact, it might have just missed the kind of hidden variable theories that Einstein is most interested in.</li>
</ol>
<p>However, <a href="/wiki/Richard_D._Gill" title="Richard D. Gill">Richard D. Gill</a> has argued that Jaynes misunderstood Bell's analysis. Gill points out that in the same conference volume in which Jaynes argues against Bell, Jaynes confesses to being extremely impressed by a short proof by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steve_Gull" title="Steve Gull">Steve Gull</a> presented at the same conference, that the singlet correlations could not be reproduced by a computer simulation of a local hidden variables theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> According to Jaynes (writing nearly 30 years after Bell's landmark contributions), it would probably take us another 30 years to fully appreciate Gull's stunning result.</p>
<p>A recent flurry of activity about implications for determinism arose with the paper: <a href="/wiki/Free_will_theorem" title="Free will theorem"><i>The Free Will Theorem</i></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> which stated "the response of a spin 1 particle to a triple experiment is free—that is to say, is not a function of properties of that part of the universe that is earlier than this response with respect to any given inertial frame."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> This theorem raised awareness of a tension between determinism fully governing an experiment (on the one hand) and Alice and Bob being free to choose any settings they like for their observations (on the other).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The philosopher David Hodgson supports this theorem as showing that determinism is <i>unscientific</i>, and that quantum mechanics allows observers (at least in some instances) the freedom to make observations of their choosing, thereby leaving the door open for free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_remarks">Final remarks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Final remarks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The violations of Bell's inequalities, due to quantum entanglement, provide near definitive demonstrations of something that was already strongly suspected: that quantum physics cannot be represented by any version of the classical picture of physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Some earlier elements that had seemed incompatible with classical pictures included <a href="/wiki/Complementarity_(physics)" title="Complementarity (physics)">complementarity</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wavefunction_collapse" title="Wavefunction collapse">wavefunction collapse</a>. The Bell violations show that no resolution of such issues can avoid the ultimate strangeness of quantum behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The EPR paper "pinpointed" the unusual properties of the <i>entangled states</i>, e.g. the above-mentioned singlet state, which is the foundation for present-day applications of quantum physics, such as <a href="/wiki/Quantum_cryptography" title="Quantum cryptography">quantum cryptography</a>; one application involves the measurement of quantum entanglement as a physical source of bits for <a href="/wiki/Michael_O._Rabin" title="Michael O. Rabin">Rabin's</a> <a href="/wiki/Oblivious_transfer" title="Oblivious transfer">oblivious transfer</a> protocol. This non-locality was originally supposed to be illusory, because the standard interpretation could easily do away with action-at-a-distance by simply assigning to each particle definite spin-states for all possible spin directions. The EPR argument was: therefore these definite states exist, therefore quantum theory is incomplete, since they do not appear in the theory. Bell's theorem showed that the "entangledness" prediction of quantum mechanics has a degree of non-locality that cannot be explained away by any local theory.</p>
<p>What is powerful about Bell's theorem is that it doesn't refer to any particular physical theory. It shows that nature violates the most general assumptions behind classical pictures, not just details of some particular models. No combination of local deterministic and local random variables can reproduce the phenomena predicted by quantum mechanics and repeatedly observed in experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell%27s_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-C.B._Parker_1994_542-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-C.B._Parker_1994_542_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-C.B._Parker_1994_542_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">C.B. Parker (1994). <i>McGraw-Hill Encyclopaedia of Physics</i> (2nd ed.). McGraw-Hill. p. 542. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-07-051400-3" title="Special:BookSources/0-07-051400-3">0-07-051400-3</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABell%27s+theorem&amp;rft.au=C.B.+Parker&amp;rft.btitle=McGraw-Hill+Encyclopaedia+of+Physics&amp;rft.date=1994&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-07-051400-3&amp;rft.pages=542&amp;rft.pub=McGraw-Hill&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Bell himself wrote: "If [a hidden variable theory] is local it will not agree with quantum mechanics, and if it agrees with quantum mechanics it will not be local. This is what the theorem says." John Bell, <i>Speakable and Unspeakable in Quantum Mechanics</i>, Cambridge University Press, 1987, p. 65.</span></li>

<p>The following are intended for general audiences.</p>

<li>J. Baggott, <i>The Meaning of Quantum Theory</i> (Oxford University Press, 1992)</li>
<li>N. David Mermin, "Is the moon there when nobody looks? Reality and the quantum theory", in <i>Physics Today</i>, April 1985, pp. 38–47.</li>
<li>Louisa Gilder, <i>The Age of Entanglement: When Quantum Physics Was Reborn</i> (New York: Alfred A. Knopf, 2008)</li>
<li>Brian Greene, <i>The Fabric of the Cosmos</i> (Vintage, 2004, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0375727205">ISBN 0-375-72720-5</a>)</li>
<li>Nick Herbert, <i>Quantum Reality: Beyond the New Physics</i> (Anchor, 1987, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0385235690">ISBN 0-385-23569-0</a>)</li>

"Bell's theorem". <i><a href="/wiki/Scholarpedia" title="Scholarpedia">Scholarpedia</a></i>. <b>6</b> (10): 8378. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2011SchpJ...6.8378G" rel="nofollow">2011SchpJ...6.8378G</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.4249%2Fscholarpedia.8378" rel="nofollow">10.4249/scholarpedia.8378</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABell%27s+theorem&amp;rft.atitle=Bell%27s+theorem&amp;rft.aufirst=Sheldon&amp;rft.aulast=Goldstein&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2011SchpJ...6.8378G&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.4249%2Fscholarpedia.8378&amp;rft.issue=10&amp;rft.jtitle=Scholarpedia&amp;rft.pages=8378&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=6"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>

Mermin's article, <cite class="citation journal">Mermin, N. D. (1981). "Bringing home the atomic world: Quantum mysteries for anybody". <i>American Journal of Physics</i>. <b>49</b> (10): 940. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1981AmJPh..49..940M" rel="nofollow">1981AmJPh..49..940M</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1119%2F1.12594" rel="nofollow">10.1119/1.12594</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABell%27s+theorem&amp;rft.atitle=Bringing+home+the+atomic+world%3A+Quantum+mysteries+for+anybody&amp;rft.aufirst=N.+D.&amp;rft.aulast=Mermin&amp;rft.date=1981&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1981AmJPh..49..940M&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1119%2F1.12594&amp;rft.issue=10&amp;rft.jtitle=American+Journal+of+Physics&amp;rft.pages=940&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=49"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>

<li><a class="external text" href="http://drchinese.com/David/Bell_Theorem_Easy_Math.htm" rel="nofollow">Bell's Theorem with Easy Math, by David R. Schneider</a>. Another simple explanation of Bell's Inequality.</li>
